# Episode 9 — Technique #8: Framing
### The same fact, two frames, two different decisions

**Block:** 1 · **Technique 8 of 20** · **Target runtime:** 13–14 min
**Anchor callback:** Campaign A — reframing smoking from "shameful" to "liberating."
**Logline:** Framing is choosing the words, the comparison, and the angle that decide what a fact *means* — and quietly decide it for you, before you weigh anything.

**Learning objectives:**
- Define *framing* and see, with a clean experiment, that wording changes choices.
- Recognize the main framing moves: gain vs. loss, the chosen baseline, what's named and what's hidden.
- Practice "reframing" any claim to see the choice clearly.

---

## COLD OPEN

[GFX: two surgeons, two waiting rooms. Surgeon A: "This operation has a 90% survival rate." Surgeon B: "This operation has a 10% death rate."]

**VO:** Two surgeons describe the exact same operation. One says it has a ninety percent survival rate. The other says it has a ten percent death rate. Identical facts. Which surgeon would you choose?

[Beat.]

**VO:** Most people pick the first — and feel calmer about the surgery — even though the two sentences are mathematically the same. That gap, between identical facts and opposite feelings, is the whole game today.

[GFX: TITLE CARD — "Technique #8: FRAMING."]

---

## SEGMENT 1 — What it is

[ON SCREEN — DEFINITION]
**Framing:** presenting the same information in a way — through wording, comparison, or emphasis — that steers how people feel about it and what they decide.

**VO:** A frame is like the edge of a photograph. The photographer chooses what's inside the frame and what's cropped out, and that choice shapes the whole picture — without altering a single thing that's actually there. Framing does the same with information: it chooses the words, the comparison, the baseline, and what to leave out.

**VO:** This isn't just folk wisdom. Psychologists Amos Tversky and Daniel Kahneman demonstrated it in a famous 1981 study. They gave people a choice about fighting a disease, described once in terms of how many lives would be *saved* and once in terms of how many would be *lost* — same numbers, same outcomes. People's choices flipped depending only on the wording. The frame, not the facts, drove the decision.

[LOWER THIRD: Framing effect — Tversky & Kahneman, 1981 ("Asian disease problem")]

---

## SEGMENT 2 — Why it works on your brain

[GFX: a single number "5%" shown two ways — "95% fat-free!" (appealing) vs. "contains 5% fat" (less appealing).]

**VO:** Your brain doesn't process raw facts; it processes facts *as presented*. We're especially sensitive to gains versus losses: research shows we feel the pain of a loss more strongly than the pleasure of an equal gain. So "don't lose this deal" hits harder than "get this deal," and "protect what you have" hits harder than "you could gain." Manipulators frame in terms of loss when they want you anxious and acting fast.

**VO:** Frames also work by setting the *baseline* — the thing you compare to. A price feels cheap next to a higher "original" price (which may have been invented for exactly that comparison). A number feels huge or tiny depending on what it's placed beside. Change the comparison and you change the meaning, without changing the number.

**VO:** And frames work by *naming.* Calling a payment a "fee" or a "surcharge" or an "investment" or a "contribution" steers your feeling about identical dollars. Calling people "rebels" or "terrorists," a policy a "reform" or a "cut" — same thing in the world, opposite reaction in your head. Whoever names it, frames it.

---

## SEGMENT 3 — In the anchor campaign

[B-ROLL: the shift from "smoking is unladylike" to "smoking is liberating."]

**VO:** Our whole cigarette campaign was, at its heart, a giant reframing. Before Bernays, the public frame for a woman smoking in the street was "improper," even "shameful." He didn't argue against that frame head-on. He built a new one and made it more attractive: smoking as *freedom*, as *equality*, as *modern*. Same act — woman, cigarette, street — wrapped in a new frame, producing the opposite feeling.

**VO:** That's framing at industrial scale. He didn't change what smoking *was*. He changed what it *meant*. And meaning is what we actually decide on.

---

## SEGMENT 4 — In the wild today

[B-ROLL: montage — "95% fat-free"; "was $200, now $99"; news chyrons describing the same event with opposite words.]

**VO:** **Advertising.** "Ninety-five percent fat-free" sells; "five percent fat" doesn't — same yogurt. "Was two hundred dollars, now ninety-nine" frames a price as a rescue from loss. "Only a dollar a day" reframes a big annual cost into a small daily one. Each is true; each is engineered to feel a certain way.

**VO:** **Politics.** Framing is the daily weather of political language. Is it an "estate tax" or a "death tax"? "Undocumented immigrants" or "illegal aliens"? A "government spending program" or an "investment in our future"? A military action with "collateral damage" or with "civilian deaths"? The underlying reality is the same; the chosen words pre-load your verdict. Whole think tanks exist to test which frame moves people, then flood the airwaves with it.

[GFX: a "same event, two chyrons" demo — a single photo with two opposite news captions.]

**VO:** Watch, too, for the *missing* frame — what got cropped out. A chart that starts its scale at a flattering number to make a small change look enormous. A statistic with no comparison, so you can't tell if it's big or small. The crop is a choice, and the choice is an argument.

---

## SEGMENT 5 — How to spot it

[GFX: "SPOT-IT" card.]

[ON SCREEN — CHECKLIST: Framing]
- **Flip it.** Restate the claim the opposite way (survival ↔ death, fat-free ↔ contains fat). Does your feeling change while the facts stay put? That's the frame talking.
- **Find the baseline.** What is this being compared to — and did someone choose that comparison to flatter the result?
- **Check the words.** Swap loaded labels for neutral ones. "Death tax" → "tax on large inheritances." Does it still land the same?
- **Ask what's cropped.** What's just outside the frame — the other side, the cost, the missing comparison?

**VO:** The master skill here is *reframing*: deliberately re-describe any claim in two or three different ways. When you can state something as a gain, a loss, and a neutral fact, you stop being a passenger in someone else's frame and start steering your own.

---

## SEGMENT 6 — Try it yourself

[GFX: "THIS WEEK'S CHALLENGE."]

**VO:** This week, catch one number or one headline and reframe it three ways: once to make it sound great, once to make it sound terrible, once flat and neutral. A "20% off" sale: great ("save twenty percent!"), terrible ("you're still paying eighty percent for something you didn't plan to buy"), neutral ("the price is X"). Doing this a few times rewires you to feel the frame as a *choice* someone made — which is the first step to making your own.

---

## RECAP + TEASE

**VO:** Framing: same facts, different words, different decision. Flip it, find the baseline, check the loaded words, and ask what's cropped out. Whoever names it, frames it — so learn to rename it yourself.

[GFX: end card — "NEXT — Technique #9: Repetition & the Illusory-Truth Effect. Why a thing repeated enough starts to feel true."]

**VO:** Next time: "It's toasted." We'll see the unsettling science showing that simply *repeating* a claim makes people believe it — even when they know better. See you there.

---

## SOURCES
- Framing effect & loss aversion: Tversky & Kahneman (1981), "The framing of decisions and the psychology of choice"; prospect theory.
- Reframing of smoking's meaning: Episode 1 sources.

*Full URLs in `SOURCES.md`.*

# Episode 10 — Technique #9: Repetition & the Illusory-Truth Effect
### Why a thing repeated enough starts to feel true

**Block:** 1 · **Technique 9 of 20** · **Target runtime:** 12–14 min
**Anchor callback:** Campaign A — the endlessly repeated slogan "It's toasted."
**Logline:** Repetition does two jobs: it makes you *like* things (the more familiar, the more comfortable) and it makes you *believe* things (the more often you hear a claim, the truer it feels) — even when you know better.

**Learning objectives:**
- Define the *mere-exposure effect* and the *illusory-truth effect*.
- Understand why familiarity feels like both safety and truth.
- Build a defense against the slogan, the talking point, and the repeated lie.

---

## COLD OPEN

[GFX: kinetic typography — "IT'S TOASTED" repeats, multiplies, fills the screen.]

**VO:** "It's toasted." For decades, a cigarette brand repeated those two words. Toasted — so what? Most tobacco is heat-treated. The phrase told you almost nothing. But it was everywhere, so often, for so long, that it lodged in millions of minds and quietly said: *this one's smoother, this one's fine.*

[Beat.]

**VO:** Repetition is the cheapest trick in the book, and one of the most powerful — because of two quirks in your brain that have been measured in the lab.

[GFX: TITLE CARD — "Technique #9: REPETITION & THE ILLUSORY-TRUTH EFFECT."]

---

## SEGMENT 1 — What it is

[ON SCREEN — DEFINITION]
**Mere-exposure effect:** we tend to *like* things more simply because they're familiar.
**Illusory-truth effect:** we tend to *believe* claims more simply because we've heard them repeated.

**VO:** Two separate effects, one engine: familiarity. The psychologist Robert Zajonc showed in 1968 that just being exposed to something repeatedly — a shape, a word, a face — makes people prefer it, with no other reason. That's the mere-exposure effect, and it's why a jingle you "hate" can still make you reach for the product.

[LOWER THIRD: Mere-exposure effect — Robert Zajonc, 1968]

**VO:** The more dangerous cousin was shown in 1977 by researchers Hasher, Goldstein, and Toppino: people rated repeated statements as more *true* than new ones — regardless of whether they actually were true. Repeat a claim, and it gains "the ring of truth." That's the illusory-truth effect.

[LOWER THIRD: Illusory-truth effect — Hasher, Goldstein & Toppino, 1977]

---

## SEGMENT 2 — Why it works on your brain

[GFX: a brain "processing" a new sentence with effort; then the same sentence again, gliding through smoothly; a meter labeled "feels true" rises.]

**VO:** Here's the unsettling mechanism. When your brain processes something easily — smoothly, without friction — it reads that ease as a signal of truth and safety. Scientists call this "fluency." A claim you've heard ten times is *easy* to process the eleventh time. Your brain mistakes that easiness for evidence: *I recognize this, so it's probably right.*

**VO:** The catch is that recognition has nothing to do with accuracy. A lie you've heard ten times also becomes easy to process — and so it, too, starts to feel true. Studies find the effect can work even on people who, when asked directly, know the correct answer. The familiar version *feels* right even as you know it's wrong. Repetition doesn't bother to defeat your knowledge; it sneaks underneath it.

**VO:** This is why "repeat a lie often enough and it becomes accepted" isn't just a cynical saying — it's a measurable feature of human minds. And it's why propagandists and advertisers alike have always loved the simple, repeated phrase.

---

## SEGMENT 3 — In the anchor campaign

[B-ROLL: vintage "It's toasted" ads across magazines, posters, radio.]

**VO:** "It's toasted" is mere exposure and illusory truth working together. The phrase was nearly content-free, but it was repeated across every medium for years. Familiarity bred liking — the brand felt like an old friend. And the vague health-flavored implication, repeated endlessly, came to *feel* true: that this cigarette was somehow smoother, gentler, safer. None of it required evidence. It only required repetition and time.

---

## SEGMENT 4 — In the wild today

[B-ROLL: montage — jingles; slogans; a politician repeating the exact same phrase in five interviews; the same false claim across many websites.]

**VO:** **Advertising.** The jingle, the slogan, the brand name said three times in a fifteen-second spot — all mere exposure. The aim isn't to convince you today; it's to make the brand so familiar that, in the half-second you choose at the shelf, your hand drifts to the one you "know."

**VO:** **Politics.** Watch how disciplined campaigns repeat an identical phrase — word for word — across every spokesperson and every interview. That's not a lack of imagination; it's engineering. They're banking the illusory-truth effect, getting the phrase to feel true through sheer repetition. The same mechanism powers misinformation online: a false claim copied across hundreds of pages and reposted thousands of times doesn't become true, but it *feels* true, because you keep meeting it. We'll see this scaled to a flood in our very last episode — the "firehose of falsehood."

[GFX: a single false claim multiplying across a grid of fake "news" sites and social posts.]

**VO:** Repetition has a particularly nasty trick: even *fact-checking* can backfire if it repeats the false claim loudly. Hearing "Myth: X is true. Fact: it isn't" can leave some people remembering only "X," now feeling familiar. The familiarity outlives the correction.

---

## SEGMENT 5 — How to spot it

[GFX: "SPOT-IT" card.]

[ON SCREEN — CHECKLIST: Repetition]
- **Familiar ≠ true.** When something feels obviously right, ask: do I believe this because of *evidence*, or just because I've heard it a thousand times?
- **Count the repeats.** Are the same exact words coming from many mouths? That's a coordinated talking point, not a spontaneous consensus.
- **Trace it once.** For a claim you "just know," try to find where it actually comes from and whether it was ever checked.
- **Beware the catchy phrase.** The more sloganlike and sticky, the more it was built to bypass thought.

**VO:** The defense is to treat the feeling of familiarity as *neutral* — a sign you've heard something before, not a sign it's correct. Separate "I recognize this" from "this is true." They feel identical inside your head. They are not the same thing.

---

## SEGMENT 6 — Try it yourself

[GFX: "THIS WEEK'S CHALLENGE."]

**VO:** This week, pick one "thing everybody knows" — a health tip, a historical "fact," a claim about a product or a group — that you've never actually checked, only absorbed. Spend ten minutes tracing it to a real source. Some will hold up. Some will dissolve into "people just keep saying it." Either way, you'll have caught your own brain mistaking familiarity for truth — the most important catch in this episode.

---

## RECAP + TEASE

**VO:** Repetition makes you like things and believe things, through familiarity and fluency — even against your own knowledge. The cure: remember that recognizing a claim is not the same as verifying it.

[GFX: end card — "NEXT — Technique #10: Engineering the Environment. Changing the world around you so the choice feels natural."]

**VO:** Next time, we finish Block 1 with the boldest move of all: when you can't change someone's mind, change their *surroundings* — like the campaign that rewrote fashion to sell a cigarette. See you there.

---

## SOURCES
- Mere-exposure effect: Zajonc (1968); Wikipedia, "Mere-exposure effect."
- Illusory-truth effect: Hasher, Goldstein & Toppino (1977); Wikipedia, "Illusory truth effect."
- "It's toasted" and slogan repetition: Episode 1 sources.

*Full URLs in `SOURCES.md`.*

# Episode 11 — Technique #10: Engineering the Environment
### When you can't change the mind, change the world around it

**Block:** 1 (finale) · **Technique 10 of 20** · **Target runtime:** 13–14 min
**Anchor callback:** Campaign A — the "green" campaign that rewrote fashion so a cigarette pack would fit in.
**Logline:** The most advanced manipulation doesn't argue with you at all. It rearranges your surroundings — defaults, choices, norms, what "everyone" seems to do — so that the desired choice feels natural, easy, and your own.

**Learning objectives:**
- See that *choice architecture* — the design of the situation — steers decisions as powerfully as any argument.
- Recognize defaults, friction, norms, and "manufactured normal" as tools.
- Learn to step outside an engineered environment and ask, "who arranged the room?"

---

## COLD OPEN

[B-ROLL: a supermarket — candy at child height by the checkout; staples at the back; the layout snaking past everything.]

**VO:** Why is the milk at the very back of the supermarket? Why is the candy exactly at a child's eye level by the till? Why do you have to walk past a thousand things to buy the five you came for?

[Beat.]

**VO:** None of that is an accident, and none of it is an *argument*. Nobody is trying to convince you of anything. They simply built the room so that you'd do what they want. That is the final, and quietest, technique in this half of the course.

[GFX: TITLE CARD — "Technique #10: ENGINEERING THE ENVIRONMENT."]

---

## SEGMENT 1 — What it is

[ON SCREEN — DEFINITION]
**Engineering the environment:** shaping the situation around a decision — the defaults, the options, the effort required, the apparent norms — so that the desired choice becomes the easy, natural, or "obvious" one.

**VO:** Every other technique so far works on your *mind* — your feelings, your trust, your sense of truth. This one works on your *world.* The persuader steps back from arguing and instead becomes an architect, designing the space in which you'll choose. Researchers call this "choice architecture": the idea that *how* options are arranged changes what people pick, every bit as much as the options themselves.

**VO:** Edward Bernays understood this a century ago. He once described his job as the "engineering of consent" — and his most astonishing campaign didn't try to change a single mind. It changed the surroundings until minds followed.

---

## SEGMENT 2 — Why it works on your brain

[GFX: a person at a door; the "easy" path glows and is wide open; the "other" path is a tangle of effort.]

**VO:** Your brain is, sensibly, lazy. Faced with a decision, it leans hard toward the path of least resistance: the default option, the thing already laid out, the choice "everyone" seems to make. We rarely fight the current of a situation; we drift with it, then tell ourselves we chose.

**VO:** So three levers quietly steer you. **Defaults:** whatever happens if you do nothing — the pre-ticked box, the auto-renewing subscription, the standard plan — wins enormously, because most people never change it. **Friction:** make the choice they want easy and the choice they don't want annoying — endless cancellation menus, buried opt-outs — and you herd people without a word. **Norms:** arrange things so a behavior looks like simply "what's done here," and people fall in line, because going against the apparent norm feels strange and costly.

**VO:** The genius — and the danger — is that this leaves no fingerprints. There's no claim to fact-check, no slogan to resist, no emotion you can name. You just find yourself having chosen what the architect wanted, feeling completely free the whole time.

---

## SEGMENT 3 — In the anchor campaign

[B-ROLL: 1930s fashion; the green Lucky Strike pack; a charity "green" ball.]

**VO:** Here's the campaign that should make your jaw drop. When research suggested some women avoided the Lucky Strike pack because its green color clashed with their wardrobes, Bernays faced a problem most marketers solve by redesigning the package. He did the opposite. He decided to make the *world* match the package.

**VO:** He set out to make green the fashionable color of the season — promoting it through fashion editors, designers, and a high-society "green" charity ball, so that green gowns, green accessories, and green décor became desirable on their own. If green was everywhere and adored, then the green cigarette pack no longer clashed; it *fit.* He didn't persuade women to overlook the color. He rearranged fashion so there was nothing to overlook.

[LOWER THIRD: The "green" campaign, c. 1934 · Source: NYT obituary of Bernays]

**VO:** That is engineering the environment in its purest form: change the surroundings, and the choice changes itself.

---

## SEGMENT 4 — In the wild today

[B-ROLL: montage — a cookie pop-up with a giant "ACCEPT ALL" and a tiny "manage settings"; an app's free trial that auto-charges; a checkout pre-ticked for insurance.]

**VO:** This technique runs your digital life.

**VO:** **Advertising and business.** The "Accept All" button is huge and bright; "Reject" is hidden three menus deep — friction, steering you to give up your data. The free trial that silently becomes a paid subscription — a default, betting you'll forget to cancel. The pre-ticked add-on at checkout, the "recommended" plan placed in the flattering middle, the store layout that walks you past temptation. None of these argue. They arrange.

**VO:** **Politics and public life.** Whether voting is easy or hard — the hours, the locations, the paperwork, the deadlines — is choice architecture, and it shapes who participates, before anyone casts a single persuasive message. Whether organ donation is opt-in or opt-out dramatically changes how many people donate, with identical underlying beliefs. The "manufactured normal" we met in the bandwagon episode lives here too: make a behavior or an opinion *appear* to be simply what your community does, and many will quietly conform.

[GFX: a "design is an argument" card — a dark-pattern cancellation flow shown as a maze.]

**VO:** When the design of a situation pushes you reliably in one direction, that design is making an argument — it's just making it with walls and buttons instead of words.

---

## SEGMENT 5 — How to spot it

[GFX: "SPOT-IT" card.]

[ON SCREEN — CHECKLIST: Engineered Environments]
- **Find the default.** What happens if I do *nothing*? Who benefits from that being the default?
- **Feel the friction.** Is one option suspiciously easy and the other annoyingly hard? Friction is a fingerprint.
- **Question the "normal."** Is this really "just how it's done," or was it arranged to look that way?
- **Ask who built the room.** Step back from the choice and look at the situation. Someone designed it. Who, and to what end?

**VO:** The defense is a mental move: rise up out of the choice and look down at the whole situation, like seeing the supermarket from above. The instant you notice that the room was *built* to make you choose a certain way, you get a sliver of freedom back — enough to take the hard path on purpose when it's the right one.

---

## SEGMENT 6 — Try it yourself

[GFX: "THIS WEEK'S CHALLENGE."]

**VO:** This week, find one default in your life and flip it on purpose. Open an app's privacy settings and actually change them. Cancel one auto-renewal you forgot about. Decline the pre-ticked extra. The point isn't the money or the data — it's the feeling of pushing against a current that was built to carry you. Notice how much effort "doing nothing differently" required. That effort *was* the manipulation.

---

## RECAP + TEASE (and end of Block 1)

**VO:** Engineering the environment: don't change the mind, change the world around it — through defaults, friction, and manufactured norms. Always ask who built the room.

**VO:** And that closes Block 1. You now have ten techniques: transfer, testimonial, bandwagon, pseudo-events, the third-party trick, glittering generalities, emotional engineering, framing, repetition, and engineering the environment. Run any advertisement through them and you'll see the machinery.

[GFX: end card — "NEXT — Episode 12, Campaign B: a modern political influence operation, fought not on the street but in your phone."]

**VO:** Next, everything changes era. We jump from the 1920s street to the modern smartphone, and from selling cigarettes to moving elections — Campaign B, and ten new techniques built for the age of the algorithm. See you there.

---

## SOURCES
- "Engineering of consent" and the green campaign: Episode 1 sources; NYT obituary of Edward Bernays.
- Choice architecture, defaults, and "nudges": the behavioral-science literature on choice architecture (e.g., Thaler & Sunstein, *Nudge*).
- Opt-in vs. opt-out organ donation effects: widely documented behavioral-economics finding.

*Full URLs in `SOURCES.md`.*

# Episode 12 — Campaign B: The 2016-Era Influence Operations
### How manipulation moved from the street into your phone

**Block:** 2 (Politics & Digital Influence) · **Type:** Example-campaign episode · **Target runtime:** 14–15 min
**Logline:** Almost a century after the Torches of Freedom march, the same human weaknesses were targeted again — but now one at a time, in private, by software. We follow two documented operations from the 2016 era and use them to map the next ten techniques.

**Learning objectives — by the end the viewer can:**
- Describe how digital influence differs from old mass advertising: targeted, private, automated, and hard to see.
- Understand two real, documented operations — a state "troll farm" and a data-profiling firm.
- Hold the crucial nuance: these tools are real *and* their power is often overstated.
- Name the ten techniques the rest of the course will explain.

---

## COLD OPEN

[B-ROLL: a thumb scrolling a phone at night; the glow on a face; posts flicking past — a meme, an outrage clip, a "patriots only" group, a heartfelt cause.]

**VO:** It's late. You're scrolling. A post slides by — it makes you angry, or proud, or afraid. You don't think much about it. Maybe you tap "like." Maybe you share it. It felt like *your* feed, *your* friends, *your* opinion forming on its own.

[Beat. The phone glow freezes.]

**VO:** But what if some of those posts were planted? What if the group of "fellow citizens" you joined was run from another continent? What if the ad you saw was crafted for your exact fears — and your neighbor, scrolling the same minute, was shown the *opposite* message?

[GFX: TITLE CARD — "MANIPULATION TECHNIQUES EXPLAINED." Subtitle: "Episode 12 — Campaign B: The 2016-Era Influence Operations."]

**VO:** Welcome to manipulation in the age of the algorithm. It uses every trick from the first half of this course — but it does it invisibly, automatically, and one person at a time.

---

## SEGMENT 1 — What changed since the cigarettes

[GFX: split timeline — 1929 "one message → millions" vs. 2016 "millions of messages → one person each."]

**VO:** Edward Bernays had to put one message in front of millions of people through newspapers everybody read. Crude, but visible — you could see the same ad your neighbor saw.

**VO:** The modern toolkit flips that. Three things changed everything. First, **data**: companies now hold thousands of details about you — what you like, click, watch, and linger on. Second, **targeting**: a message can be aimed at just *you*, or just people like you, and shown to no one else. Third, **automation**: software, not people, decides what billions of us see, and fake accounts can imitate a crowd at the press of a button.

**VO:** The result is persuasion you mostly can't see. There's no shared public ad to point at and argue about. The manipulation happens in private, in your pocket, tuned to your weaknesses. To learn how it works, we'll look at two operations from around 2016 that were investigated in extraordinary public detail.

---

## SEGMENT 2 — Operation one: the troll farm

[B-ROLL: a nondescript office building; rows of computers; shift workers.]
[LOWER THIRD: The "Internet Research Agency," St. Petersburg, Russia]

**VO:** In St. Petersburg, Russia, an organization called the Internet Research Agency ran what investigators described as a "troll farm" — a building full of paid employees whose job was to pretend to be other people online. In 2018, the U.S. Special Counsel Robert Mueller formally accused thirteen Russian individuals and three organizations of a scheme to interfere in American politics.

[LOWER THIRD: Mueller indictment, 16 Feb 2018 · Source: U.S. House Intelligence Committee]

**VO:** Here's the part that matters most for our purposes, and it's confirmed in *bipartisan* U.S. Senate reports — backed by both major parties. The stated goal wasn't to push one tidy message. It was to **sow discord** — to widen the cracks already in society.

[LOWER THIRD: Bipartisan Senate Intelligence Committee reports, Dec 2018]

**VO:** So the operation played *every* side at once. It ran accounts and pages posing as angry partisans on the right *and* as activists on the left. It built pages targeting Black Americans, pages targeting religious conservatives, pages for immigrants and against immigration — sometimes organizing real protesters on opposite sides of the same street, all from the same office. The aim wasn't to win an argument. It was to make Americans hate each other more.

[GFX: a single hidden hand puppeteering two angry crowds facing off; both strings lead back to one desk.]

**VO:** Sit with how different that is from a normal campaign. A normal campaign wants you to agree with it. This operation didn't care what you believed — only that you believed it *furiously*, and trusted your fellow citizens a little less.

---

## SEGMENT 3 — Operation two: the profiling firm

[B-ROLL: a personality quiz on a phone; data points streaming off a Facebook profile.]
[LOWER THIRD: Cambridge Analytica]

**VO:** The second operation is about *data*. A political consulting firm called Cambridge Analytica became the symbol of a new fear: that your social-media life could be mined to manipulate your vote.

**VO:** It worked, roughly, like this. A researcher built a personality quiz app. The people who took it didn't just hand over their own answers — through the social network's settings at the time, the app also scooped up data on their *friends*, who never agreed to anything. By this route, the data of up to eighty-seven million people was harvested. The firm's pitch was that it could sort voters by personality type and hit each with tailor-made messages — what's called "psychographic" targeting.

[LOWER THIRD: Up to 87 million profiles harvested · Whistleblower: Christopher Wylie, 2018]

**VO:** The firm worked for political campaigns, and when a whistleblower named Christopher Wylie went public in 2018, it became a global scandal about privacy and manipulation.

---

## SEGMENT 4 — The most important lesson: don't believe the hype

[GFX: a big "REALITY CHECK" stamp.]

**VO:** Now stop — because here is the single most valuable thing in this episode, and it's the opposite of what the headlines screamed.

**VO:** The scary story was that a magic algorithm read voters' souls and flipped an election. The careful evidence says: probably not. Reporting later noted that the firm itself admitted the fancy "psychographic" method wasn't actually used in the campaign people most associate it with. Britain's privacy regulator, after investigating, found the firm's tools weren't the world-beating weapon advertised, and found no evidence it misused data in the Brexit vote. Academic researchers concluded the firm was broadly similar to its ordinary competitors and likely far less effective than the legend.

[LOWER THIRD: Sources: Fortune (2018); UK Information Commissioner's Office (2020); academic analysis (Jakee & Fink, 2024)]

**VO:** Why hammer this? Because **overstating the manipulator's power is itself a kind of manipulation** — and a trap for *us*, the people trying to stay alert. If you believe the enemy is an unbeatable mind-control machine, you give up. You stop trusting your own judgment. You assume every opinion you don't like must be the work of bots and algorithms. That helplessness and that paranoia are *exactly* the discord these operations wanted to create. The firm, the press, and even the operations themselves all benefited from the myth of their own omnipotence.

**VO:** So hold two truths at once, the grown-up way: these techniques are real, they're used against you, and you should learn them — *and* they are not magic, your mind is not putty, and naming them takes most of their power away.

---

## SEGMENT 5 — How it was targeted

[GFX: section header — "THE PLAYBOOK."]

**VO:** Strip away the technology and you'll recognize old friends from Block 1, plus new digital muscle. Here's the shared playbook of modern influence operations.

**VO:** **Find the fault lines.** Every society has raw nerves — race, religion, immigration, region, identity. Operators don't invent these divisions; they find the real ones and pour accelerant on them.

**VO:** **Pose as one of us.** Instead of an obvious outsider, the message comes from accounts pretending to be ordinary members of your own group — the third-party technique from Block 1, now at the scale of thousands of fake people.

**VO:** **Aim for the strongest emotion, especially anger.** Content that enrages spreads fastest, because furious people share. So the operation manufactures outrage on purpose and lets *us* do the distributing for free.

**VO:** **Target narrowly, hide widely.** Different people get different — even opposite — messages, privately, so no one sees the whole game.

[GFX: a network map — fault lines glowing, fake "neighbor" accounts seeded along them, outrage rippling outward, real users resharing.]

---

## SEGMENT 6 — The ten techniques (your roadmap)

[GFX: a 10-item "season map" builds, one line at a time, each with its episode number.]

**VO:** Here's where Block 2 goes. Ten techniques, one short episode each.

[ON SCREEN — numbered list, revealed line by line.]

**VO:**
11. **Microtargeting** — aiming a tailor-made message at you alone, based on your data.
12. **Bots and sockpuppets** — fake accounts faking a crowd.
13. **Manufactured outrage** — engineering anger because anger spreads.
14. **Us-versus-them and scapegoating** — turning a group into the enemy.
15. **Fear appeals and threat inflation** — making a danger feel bigger so you'll act.
16. **Name-calling and demonization** — destroying a person instead of answering them.
17. **The false dilemma** — pretending there are only two options, theirs and disaster.
18. **Whataboutism** — dodging a charge by pointing at someone else's sins.
19. **Dog whistles** — coded words that mean one thing to most, another to a target group.
20. **The firehose of falsehood** — drowning the truth in a flood of lies.

**VO:** Learn these ten and you'll have the second half of the modern persuasion toolkit — the half built for screens.

---

## SEGMENT 7 — Your tool, upgraded for the feed

[GFX: the "SPOT-IT" card, "digital edition."]

**VO:** Take the five questions from Episode 1 and add three for the digital age:

[ON SCREEN — CHECKLIST]
1. **Who really posted this — and are they who they claim?**
2. **Why is this in front of *me*, right now?** (Who chose to show it to me?)
3. **Is this designed to make me *share* while angry?**
4. *(plus the original five: who pays, what feeling, what action, what's missing, would it hold up tomorrow.)*

**VO:** Above all, remember the reality check: the goal of much of this is not to make you believe a specific lie. It's to make you angrier, lonelier, and more certain that the other half of your neighbors are monsters. The most radical thing you can do — the thing the troll farm least wants — is to stay curious, stay calm, and refuse to hate on command.

---

## RECAP + TEASE

**VO:** Campaign B in one sentence: *the old tricks, now invisible, automated, and aimed at you alone — powerful, but not magic.* The cure starts with seeing the machinery.

[GFX: end card — "NEXT — Technique #11: Microtargeting. Why your phone shows you a different ad than your neighbor."]

**VO:** Next time: how your everyday data becomes a map of your weak spots, and why the message aimed at you was never meant for anyone else. See you there.

[GFX: standard disclaimer card.]
[ON SCREEN: "This episode analyzes documented operations to teach techniques. Sources, including bipartisan government reports, are listed below and in the description."]

---

## SOURCES (on-screen citations for this episode)

- Internet Research Agency, the Mueller indictment (13 individuals + 3 organizations, Feb 16, 2018), and the goal of sowing discord: U.S. House Permanent Select Committee on Intelligence; "Mueller report" (overview).
- Operations playing all sides and targeting many groups: bipartisan U.S. Senate Select Committee on Intelligence reports (Dec 2018); NPR and Privacy International summaries.
- Cambridge Analytica, the quiz app, up to 87 million profiles, Christopher Wylie: Wikipedia, "Facebook–Cambridge Analytica data scandal"; The Guardian; BBC News.
- The effectiveness nuance (tools likely overstated): Fortune (2018); UK Information Commissioner's Office findings (2020); Jakee & Fink (2024).

# Episode 13 — Technique #11: Microtargeting & Psychographic Profiling
### Why your phone shows you a different message than your neighbor

**Block:** 2 · **Technique 11 of 20** · **Target runtime:** 13–14 min
**Anchor callback:** Campaign B — Cambridge Analytica's promise to sort voters by personality and message each one differently.
**Logline:** Microtargeting uses your data to aim a custom-built message at you alone — so each person sees the version most likely to move *them*, and no one sees the whole picture.

**Learning objectives:**
- Define *microtargeting* and *psychographic profiling* in plain terms.
- Understand why "a different message for everyone" is more dangerous than one shared lie.
- Keep the nuance: targeting is real and routine, but its mind-reading powers are often oversold.

---

## COLD OPEN

[B-ROLL: two neighbors on their porches, each looking at a phone; thought bubbles show two completely different political ads for the same candidate.]

**VO:** Two neighbors. Same street, same election, same candidate. One sees an ad about taxes and freedom. The other sees an ad — from the *same* campaign — about safety and fear. Neither knows the other got a different pitch. Neither can compare notes. Each thinks they've seen "the campaign."

[Beat.]

**VO:** That's microtargeting. And it quietly breaks something we used to take for granted: that we were all, more or less, looking at the same thing.

[GFX: TITLE CARD — "Technique #11: MICROTARGETING & PSYCHOGRAPHIC PROFILING."]

---

## SEGMENT 1 — What it is

[ON SCREEN — DEFINITION]
**Microtargeting:** using data about individuals to deliver narrowly tailored messages to small, specific groups — or to single people.
**Psychographic profiling:** sorting people not just by age or location, but by *personality, values, and emotional triggers*.

**VO:** Old advertising was a megaphone: one message, blasted at everyone. Microtargeting is a thousand whispers, each tuned to a different ear. The advertiser starts with data — your age, your location, the pages you follow, the things you've clicked, bought, watched, and paused on — and uses it to guess what will move *you* specifically.

**VO:** Psychographics goes a step further, trying to sort you by personality: Are you anxious or adventurous? Do you respond to threats or to hope? The pitch of firms like the one in our Campaign B episode was that they could find the emotional key to each personality type and turn it.

---

## SEGMENT 2 — Why it works on your brain

[GFX: a "mirror" that reflects back exactly what a person already fears or wants.]

**VO:** Microtargeting works for two reasons. The first is simple relevance: a message about your specific worry lands harder than a generic one. If a campaign knows you're worried about your job, an ad about jobs will hit you where you live.

**VO:** The second reason is the dangerous one, and it's about what *you* can't see. When everyone saw the same billboard, lies were riskier — someone would call them out, and the contradiction was public. Microtargeting removes the witnesses. A campaign can tell one group one thing and another group the opposite, and because the messages are private, the contradiction never surfaces. There's no town square anymore, just millions of separate rooms. Accountability depends on us seeing the same thing — and that's exactly what this technique dissolves.

**VO:** It also exploits a blind spot: we each assume our feed reflects reality, not a curated, personalized funhouse mirror built to reflect our own triggers back at us.

---

## SEGMENT 3 — In the anchor campaign

[B-ROLL: the personality quiz; data streaming off profiles; "OCEAN" personality letters.]

**VO:** Cambridge Analytica is the poster child. Its sales pitch was pure psychographic microtargeting: harvest millions of profiles, score each person on personality traits, then serve each personality the message most likely to move it — the fearful get fear, the proud get pride.

[LOWER THIRD: Cambridge Analytica's psychographic pitch · Source: Campaign B episode]

**VO:** But remember our reality check. The careful evidence suggests the firm's mind-reading was more sales pitch than science — reportedly not even used as advertised in the campaign it's famous for, and judged by researchers to be roughly as good (or as limited) as ordinary targeting. So the lesson here is *two-sided*: microtargeting absolutely happens and is worth guarding against — *and* the specific fantasy of an algorithm that reads your soul and overrides your will is mostly hype. The everyday, boring version — "show job ads to worried workers" — is real and powerful enough.

---

## SEGMENT 4 — In the wild today

[B-ROLL: montage — an online store "recommended for you"; a political ad library; an insurance quote that changes by zip code.]

**VO:** **Advertising.** This is the engine of the modern internet. The reason an ad seems to "follow you around" after you looked at a product once is microtargeting. The reason two people get different prices for the same flight or insurance can be data-driven targeting. It's mostly mundane — and mostly invisible.

**VO:** **Politics.** Campaigns buy data on voters and serve different ads to different micro-audiences: one message to suburban parents, another to young renters, another to a single neighborhood. Done openly, some of this is just efficient outreach. Done in the dark, it lets a campaign be many contradictory things to many people, and to slip inflammatory messages to small groups where few will see and challenge them — the "dark ads" problem that pushed several countries to demand public ad libraries.

[GFX: a "shared billboard" (everyone sees it, it's accountable) vs. "a thousand private whispers" (no one can compare).]

**VO:** The healthy response isn't to panic that you're being mind-controlled. It's to remember that *what you see is a selection made for you* — and to go looking, sometimes, at what other people are being shown.

---

## SEGMENT 5 — How to spot it

[GFX: "SPOT-IT" card.]

[ON SCREEN — CHECKLIST: Microtargeting]
- **"Why am I seeing this?"** Many platforms have an option that literally tells you why an ad was shown to you. Use it.
- **Assume a tailored version.** If it's an ad, someone chose to show it to *people like you*. Ask what version your opposite number is getting.
- **Compare feeds.** Talk to people unlike you about what ads and posts *they* see. The differences are revealing.
- **Resist the "soul-reading" panic.** Targeting nudges; it doesn't hypnotize. Don't trade naivety for helplessness.

**VO:** The deepest defense is to deliberately step outside your personalized bubble: read sources you wouldn't be shown, seek out the message aimed at the *other* group. You can't un-rig your own feed entirely, but you can refuse to mistake it for the whole world.

---

## SEGMENT 6 — Try it yourself

[GFX: "THIS WEEK'S CHALLENGE."]

**VO:** This week, do two things. First, find the "why am I seeing this ad?" button on a platform you use and read what it says about how you've been categorized — it's often startling. Second, ask one person whose life differs from yours — a different age, place, or politics — to show you the ads and posts on *their* feed. Seeing how differently the same world is presented to the two of you is the whole lesson, learned in five minutes.

---

## RECAP + TEASE

**VO:** Microtargeting: a custom message for everyone, seen by no one else, so contradictions never get caught. Ask "why am I seeing this?" and go look at what others are shown. Real — but not mind control.

[GFX: end card — "NEXT — Technique #12: Bots, Sockpuppets & Manufactured Crowds. How a handful of operators fake a roaring mob."]

**VO:** Next time: the fake accounts that pretend to be thousands of real people — and how to tell a movement from a machine. See you there.

---

## SOURCES
- Microtargeting and psychographics in 2016: see Campaign B (Episode 12) sources — Cambridge Analytica scandal coverage.
- Effectiveness nuance: Fortune (2018); UK Information Commissioner's Office (2020); Jakee & Fink (2024).
- "Dark ads" and ad-transparency responses: public ad-library initiatives by major platforms following 2018 investigations.

*Full URLs in `SOURCES.md`.*

# Episode 14 — Technique #12: Bots, Sockpuppets & Manufactured Crowds
### How a handful of operators fake a roaring mob

**Block:** 2 · **Technique 12 of 20** · **Target runtime:** 13–14 min
**Anchor callback:** Campaign B — the troll farm where paid workers ran armies of fake "American" accounts.
**Logline:** This is astroturfing for the digital age: fake accounts — automated bots and human-run "sockpuppets" — impersonate ordinary people to fake a crowd, hijack trends, and make a fringe view look like the popular will.

**Learning objectives:**
- Tell apart *bots* (automated) and *sockpuppets* (fake personas run by people).
- Understand why a fake crowd works even when you suspect some accounts are fake.
- Spot the tells of an inauthentic account or coordinated campaign.

---

## COLD OPEN

[B-ROLL: a single person at a desk with dozens of phones and browser windows, each logged into a different "person's" account.]

**VO:** Picture one person at one desk, running fifty different accounts. Fifty names, fifty profile photos, fifty "personalities" — a worried mom, a proud veteran, a fired-up student. Now picture a building full of those people. Now picture software that runs not fifty but fifty *thousand* accounts automatically.

[Beat.]

**VO:** That's how you manufacture a crowd out of almost no one. And a crowd, as we learned back in Block 1, is one of the most persuasive things on earth.

[GFX: TITLE CARD — "Technique #12: BOTS, SOCKPUPPETS & MANUFACTURED CROWDS."]

---

## SEGMENT 1 — What it is

[ON SCREEN — DEFINITION]
**Bot:** an automated account that posts, likes, and shares by software, not a person.
**Sockpuppet:** a fake identity operated by a real person to hide who's really talking.
**Coordinated inauthentic behavior:** many fake accounts acting together to fake a grassroots movement.

**VO:** Remember astroturfing from Block 1 — fake grassroots, the front group in a friendly mask? Bots and sockpuppets are astroturfing turned into an industrial machine. Instead of one fake "citizens' group," you create thousands of fake *citizens*, each looking like an individual, all secretly working for the same hidden hand.

**VO:** The goal is to fake three things: **numbers** (make a view look popular), **consensus** (make it look like "everyone" agrees), and **momentum** (push something to "trend" so the algorithms — and then real people — amplify it).

---

## SEGMENT 2 — Why it works on your brain

[GFX: the "everyone is staring at the sky" crowd from Episode 4 returns — but now half the figures are revealed as cardboard cutouts.]

**VO:** This technique is a parasite on the social-proof instinct from Episode 4. Your brain reads "lots of people are saying this" as "this must be true and normal." Bots and sockpuppets feed that instinct junk food: a manufactured "lots of people."

**VO:** Here's the cruel twist. It works *even if you doubt some accounts are fake.* You might scroll past an angry thread thinking "half of these are probably bots" — but the sheer volume still shifts your sense of where "most people" stand. It makes a fringe position feel mainstream and a mainstream position feel embattled. And it bullies the social-proof of silence: when a topic looks swarmed by one side, real people with other views go quiet, which makes the fake majority look even more real.

**VO:** There's also a sneaky second job. Some fake accounts don't push a view at all; they just pollute. They flood every conversation with noise, insults, and nonsense until honest people give up and leave. Exhaustion is a goal, not a side effect.

---

## SEGMENT 3 — In the anchor campaign

[B-ROLL: the troll farm; fake "American" profile pages; a staged online "movement."]

**VO:** Our Campaign B troll farm was a sockpuppet factory. Investigators documented paid employees running large numbers of fake accounts posing as ordinary Americans across the political spectrum — building pages, growing followers, and even using those fake personas to organize real-world events. The accounts looked homegrown. The hand behind them was foreign and coordinated.

[LOWER THIRD: Coordinated fake accounts run by the Internet Research Agency · Source: bipartisan Senate Intelligence Committee reports]

**VO:** Crucially, the point wasn't one message. It was to *be* the crowd on every side — to manufacture the appearance of furious, mobilized masses where there were really just shift workers and scripts. A fake crowd, pointed at a real one.

---

## SEGMENT 4 — In the wild today

[B-ROLL: montage — a product with thousands of suspiciously similar 5-star reviews; a hashtag "trending" from a tiny cluster of accounts; identical replies under a post.]

**VO:** **Advertising and reputation.** Fake reviews and fake followers are a paid service. A brand-new product can buy a thousand glowing reviews; an "influencer" can buy a following; a company can flood a rival's page with complaints. The crowd you're reading may be for hire.

**VO:** **Politics and public debate.** Bot and sockpuppet networks push hashtags to fake "trends," swarm opponents, and make tiny factions look like national movements. They're used by states, by campaigns, by activist groups, by anyone who wants to look bigger than they are. After 2016, platforms began removing networks of fake accounts by the millions and labeling it "coordinated inauthentic behavior" — an admission of just how routine it had become.

[GFX: a "trending" topic zooms out to reveal it was driven by a small cluster of accounts each posting hundreds of times.]

**VO:** None of this means everyone who disagrees with you is a bot — that accusation is itself a manipulation, a lazy way to dismiss real people. The skill is narrower: learn to ask whether a *suspiciously sudden, suspiciously uniform* surge of "public opinion" is actually a manufactured crowd.

---

## SEGMENT 5 — How to spot it

[GFX: "SPOT-IT" card.]

[ON SCREEN — CHECKLIST: Manufactured Crowds]
- **Check the account, not just the post.** Brand-new? No real history? A stock or stolen photo? A name like a word plus eight digits? Posts at all hours, nonstop? Tells of a bot.
- **Look for uniformity.** Many accounts posting near-identical wording at near-identical times is coordination, not coincidence.
- **Sudden surges.** A topic that goes from nowhere to "everywhere" in an hour deserves suspicion, not reflex sharing.
- **Volume vs. variety.** A real movement has messy, varied voices. A fake one repeats a few scripts.

**VO:** And the master move: judge ideas by *evidence*, not by *crowd size*. If you never let "lots of accounts agree" count as proof in the first place, you've already disarmed the entire technique — bots included.

---

## SEGMENT 6 — Try it yourself

[GFX: "THIS WEEK'S CHALLENGE."]

**VO:** This week, next time something is "trending" or a post has a swarm of identical replies, spend two minutes clicking into a few of the accounts. When was it created? What else does it post? Is there a real human history, or just a flag emoji, a slogan, and a wall of rage? You won't always be sure — and that's fine. The goal is just to break the automatic reflex of treating a loud crowd as a real one.

---

## RECAP + TEASE

**VO:** Bots and sockpuppets: a manufactured crowd that hijacks your social-proof instinct, fakes consensus, and exhausts honest voices. Check the accounts, distrust sudden uniform surges, and judge by evidence, not headcount.

[GFX: end card — "NEXT — Technique #13: Manufactured Outrage. Why the maddest post always finds you."]

**VO:** Next time: why anger is the fuel of the modern internet, and how outrage is manufactured precisely because *you* will spread it for free. See you there.

---

## SOURCES
- Troll farm / fake-account operations: bipartisan U.S. Senate Intelligence Committee reports (2018); Mueller indictment. See Episode 12 sources.
- Astroturfing lineage: Episode 6 (term coined by Sen. Lloyd Bentsen, 1985).
- "Coordinated inauthentic behavior" removals: platform transparency reporting following 2018.

*Full URLs in `SOURCES.md`.*

# Episode 15 — Technique #13: Manufactured Outrage & Emotional Virality
### Why the maddest post always finds you

**Block:** 2 · **Technique 13 of 20** · **Target runtime:** 13–14 min
**Anchor callback:** Campaign B — content engineered to enrage, because furious people share.
**Logline:** Outrage spreads faster than calm, so manipulators (and the algorithms that profit from your attention) manufacture anger on purpose — and turn you into the unpaid delivery system.

**Learning objectives:**
- Understand why anger and moral outrage travel farther and faster online.
- See how outrage is *engineered* — and how algorithms reward it without anyone "deciding" to.
- Learn the pause that stops you from becoming a distributor.

---

## COLD OPEN

[B-ROLL: a feed scrolling calmly past a sunset photo, a recipe... then STOPS hard on a rage-bait headline. Thumb hovers over "share."]

**VO:** Be honest. When did you last share something because it was *true and useful* — versus because it made your blood boil and you needed someone else to feel it too? That heat in your chest, that "can you BELIEVE this" — that's the most valuable fuel on the internet. And a lot of it is manufactured.

[GFX: TITLE CARD — "Technique #13: MANUFACTURED OUTRAGE & EMOTIONAL VIRALITY."]

---

## SEGMENT 1 — What it is

[ON SCREEN — DEFINITION]
**Manufactured outrage:** deliberately provoking anger or moral indignation to capture attention and drive sharing.
**Emotional virality:** the tendency of high-arousal emotional content — especially anger — to spread far and fast.

**VO:** Researchers who study how things spread online keep finding the same pattern: content that triggers "high-arousal" emotions — awe, but especially anger and outrage — gets shared more than calm or sad content. Moral outrage in particular is rocket fuel, because sharing it feels righteous: you're not just venting, you're *warning* people, taking a stand.

**VO:** Manufacturers of outrage exploit this. They craft headlines, clips, and posts engineered to make you furious — often by stripping context, picking the worst possible example, or staging a provocation — because anger is the emotion most likely to make you hit "share."

---

## SEGMENT 2 — Why it works on your brain

[GFX: a brain with an "anger" button wired directly to a "share" button, bypassing a dim "think" bulb.]

**VO:** Anger is an action emotion. Fear makes you freeze or flee; sadness makes you withdraw; but anger makes you *do* something — and online, the easiest something is to share, comment, and pile on. Outrage also shuts down the slow, careful brain. When you're furious, you don't fact-check; you forward.

**VO:** Then there's the machinery underneath. The systems that decide what you see are mostly built to maximize one thing: your engagement — time, clicks, reactions, shares. Outrage maximizes engagement. So even with *no* villain in the loop, the algorithm learns, all by itself, that the angriest content keeps you scrolling, and serves you more of it. The platform isn't "trying" to enrage you any more than water "tries" to flow downhill. It just rewards whatever holds attention, and rage holds attention.

**VO:** Put a deliberate manipulator on top of that automatic system and you get a flywheel: they make the bait, the algorithm spreads the bait, you share the bait, which teaches the algorithm to spread more. Your genuine, justified anger becomes someone else's distribution network.

---

## SEGMENT 3 — In the anchor campaign

[B-ROLL: the troll farm; divisive memes designed to enrage both sides.]

**VO:** Our Campaign B operation understood this perfectly. Its goal — sow discord — is just manufactured outrage at national scale. Investigators found its most successful content wasn't subtle persuasion; it was inflammatory memes and posts designed to enrage people on every side, about the rawest issues in the society. The operators didn't need to convince you of anything. They needed you angry enough to share — at which point real users, not bots, carried the poison the rest of the way.

[LOWER THIRD: Divisive, outrage-driven content amplified by real users · Source: Senate Intelligence Committee reports]

**VO:** That's the chilling efficiency of it: the operation supplied the spark; *we* supplied the fire.

---

## SEGMENT 4 — In the wild today

[B-ROLL: montage — rage-bait headlines; a pundit deliberately saying something outrageous; a clip stripped of context going viral; "you won't believe what they said."]

**VO:** **Media and advertising.** "Rage-bait" is now a content strategy. Some commentators and pages make a living saying the most provocative possible thing, because outrage — even outrage from people who *hate* them — feeds the algorithm and pays the bills. The angry quote-share from an opponent is worth as much as a fan's applause. Even some brands court controversy on purpose, betting that the fury sells.

**VO:** **Politics.** Outrage is mobilization. Campaigns and partisan media learn that fear and anger get people to donate, vote, and turn out far more reliably than calm policy. So the incentive is to keep supporters perpetually enraged at the other side — to find or manufacture a fresh outrage every single day. The clip that "proves" the other side is monstrous is often missing the context that would deflate it.

[GFX: a "context-ectomy" demo — a sentence shown in full (reasonable), then with the middle cut out (outrageous).]

**VO:** Watch especially for the *context-ectomy*: a real quote or clip with the surrounding context surgically removed, so a defensible statement becomes a damning one. And watch for outrage about outrage — the endless loop of "look how angry THEY are," which keeps everyone angry forever.

---

## SEGMENT 5 — How to spot it

[GFX: "SPOT-IT" card.]

[ON SCREEN — CHECKLIST: Manufactured Outrage]
- **Feel the spike.** A sudden surge of fury is a signal to *slow down*, not speed up. The stronger the urge to share immediately, the more important the pause.
- **Ask: who profits from my anger?** A page, a campaign, an algorithm? Outrage is rarely free of someone's benefit.
- **Hunt for the missing context.** Is this a clip or quote stripped of what came before and after? Find the full version before reacting.
- **"Too perfect to be true."** If something is *exactly* engineered to enrage your side about their side, treat it as bait until proven otherwise.

**VO:** The single most powerful defense in this entire course is almost embarrassingly simple: **the pause.** Before you share something that made you furious, wait. Ten minutes, an hour, a night. Outrage is built to beat your slow brain by a few seconds. Give your slow brain those seconds back, and most manufactured outrage dies in your hands.

---

## SEGMENT 6 — Try it yourself

[GFX: "THIS WEEK'S CHALLENGE."]

**VO:** This week, adopt one rule: *I do not share anything while angry.* When a post enrages you and you feel the urge to pass it on, screenshot the urge in your mind, wait until the next day, and then decide. Keep a tally of how many "must-share" outrages still seem worth sharing after the wait. Most people are stunned by how short that list becomes.

---

## RECAP + TEASE

**VO:** Manufactured outrage: anger spreads fastest, so it's made on purpose and amplified automatically, turning you into the delivery system. The cure is the pause — never share while furious.

[GFX: end card — "NEXT — Technique #14: Us-vs-Them & Scapegoating. How to turn neighbors into enemies."]

**VO:** Next time: the ancient, dangerous pleasure of dividing the world into "us" and "them," and how a scapegoat is built. See you there.

---

## SOURCES
- Anger/high-arousal emotions and online sharing: research on emotional virality and moral-emotional content spread (e.g., Berger & Milkman; Brady et al.).
- Divisive, outrage-driven content in 2016 operations: Senate Intelligence Committee reports. See Episode 12 sources.
- Engagement-optimizing algorithms: widely documented platform-design analyses.

*Full URLs in `SOURCES.md`.*

# Episode 16 — Technique #14: Us-vs-Them & Scapegoating
### How to turn neighbors into enemies

**Block:** 2 · **Technique 14 of 20** · **Target runtime:** 13–14 min
**Anchor callback:** Campaign B — pitting groups against each other and inflaming every identity.
**Logline:** Splitting the world into a virtuous "us" and a threatening "them" is one of the oldest and most dangerous moves in persuasion. Add a scapegoat — a group blamed for your problems — and you have a tool that has driven everything from sales to atrocities.

**Learning objectives:**
- Understand why the brain forms "us" and "them" almost instantly, and how that's exploited.
- Define *scapegoating* and recognize the blame-shift.
- Learn to notice when you're being recruited into a tribe against a manufactured enemy.

---

## COLD OPEN

[B-ROLL: two groups of strangers handed red or blue wristbands at random; within minutes, they cluster, eye each other warily.]

**VO:** Hand a room of strangers colored wristbands at random — red, blue — and tell them nothing else. Within minutes, people favor their own color. They trust the reds more, share with the reds more, and start to find the blues a little suspicious. No history. No reason. Just a color, assigned by a coin flip.

[Beat.]

**VO:** Psychologists have run versions of this experiment for decades. The lesson is unsettling: the line between "us" and "them" is so easy to draw that you can do it with nothing at all. Which means anyone who *wants* to divide you has very little work to do.

[GFX: TITLE CARD — "Technique #14: US-VS-THEM & SCAPEGOATING."]

---

## SEGMENT 1 — What it is

[ON SCREEN — DEFINITION]
**Us-vs-them (in-group / out-group):** framing an issue as a struggle between your good group and a threatening other group.
**Scapegoating:** blaming a person or group for problems they didn't cause, to give anger a target.

**VO:** "Us-vs-them" turns a question of *ideas* into a question of *loyalty*. Once you belong to a side, you stop weighing arguments and start defending your team. Scapegoating adds a villain: it takes complicated problems — the economy, crime, your own hard luck — and pins them on a group that's easy to resent. Complex fear becomes simple blame, and blame feels good. It's clean. It points somewhere.

**VO:** The scapegoat is usually a group that's already disliked, already a minority, or already "other" in some way — because the blame has to feel plausible to the people you're recruiting.

---

## SEGMENT 2 — Why it works on your brain

[GFX: the red/blue wristband crowd; a "social identity" meter lighting up.]

**VO:** For almost all of human history, your group *was* your survival. The tribe fed you, defended you, and exiled you to die if you betrayed it. So evolution built us to crave belonging and to treat outsiders with caution. Researchers studying "social identity" found that we draw part of our very sense of self from the groups we belong to — which means an attack on the group feels like an attack on *you*.

[LOWER THIRD: On group identity and in-group bias — social-identity research (Tajfel and others)]

**VO:** Manipulators exploit three things this creates. **Belonging:** the warm pull to be part of the in-group. **Loyalty:** once you're in, disagreeing with the group feels like betrayal, so you self-censor. And **threat:** nothing unites a group like a common enemy. If a leader can convince you that "they" are coming for you, you'll forgive your own side almost anything and follow almost anywhere.

**VO:** Scapegoating works because blame is psychologically easier than complexity. "The economy is shaped by global forces, technology, and a hundred trade-offs" is true and unsatisfying. "*They* did this to you" is false and delicious. It hands you a villain, absolves you of any role, and gives your fear somewhere to go.

---

## SEGMENT 3 — In the anchor campaign

[B-ROLL: the troll farm; opposing groups organized against each other.]

**VO:** Our Campaign B operation was, at its core, an us-vs-them machine. Its goal wasn't to make Americans love Russia; it was to make Americans loathe *each other*. It built fake communities for many different identities and then fed each one content portraying the others as dangerous, contemptuous enemies. It even arranged for opposing groups to protest the same event at the same place — manufacturing the visual of a nation at war with itself.

[LOWER THIRD: Inflaming many group identities against each other · Source: Senate Intelligence Committee reports]

**VO:** Notice it didn't pick one scapegoat. It handed *every* group a scapegoat — each other. That's the dark genius: us-vs-them doesn't need a true enemy, just a believable one, multiplied.

---

## SEGMENT 4 — In the wild today

[B-ROLL: montage — "real fans vs. sellouts" marketing; political ads casting the other side as an existential threat; "those people" rhetoric.]

**VO:** **Advertising.** Even brands play it: "Are you a [our brand] person or one of *them*?" Building tribal identity around a product — phone camps, car camps, console camps — turns customers into defenders who'll argue with strangers for free.

**VO:** **Politics.** This is the heavy artillery. Listen for the slide from "I disagree with their policy" to "they are not real members of this nation," "they hate you," "they want to destroy our way of life." When opponents stop being fellow citizens with bad ideas and become an existential enemy, persuasion has been replaced by mobilization. And scapegoating recurs in every era and on every side: blame the immigrants, blame the elites, blame the foreigners, blame the minority — whoever is convenient for the fear of the moment.

[GFX: a dial that slides from "I disagree with them" → "they're wrong" → "they're bad" → "they're the enemy." The last setting glows red.]

**VO:** The reliable warning sign is *dehumanizing* language — describing a group as vermin, as a disease, as a flood or an infestation. That language has preceded the worst events in human history. It is never a small thing, no matter who says it about whom.

---

## SEGMENT 5 — How to spot it

[GFX: "SPOT-IT" card.]

[ON SCREEN — CHECKLIST: Us-vs-Them]
- **Loyalty over logic?** Are you being asked to *defend a side*, or to weigh an argument?
- **One simple villain for a complex problem?** Real problems have many causes. A single human scapegoat is a red flag.
- **Dehumanizing words?** Vermin, disease, flood, infestation, "not really one of us." Treat these as alarms.
- **Are "they" allowed any humanity?** Manipulators paint the out-group as uniformly evil. Reality is never that tidy.

**VO:** The defense is a deliberate act of imagination: picture a *specific, decent person* in the group being demonized — and ask whether the sweeping accusation really fits them. Stereotypes survive only at a distance. Individuals break them. Insisting on the individual is how you refuse the recruitment.

---

## SEGMENT 6 — Try it yourself

[GFX: "THIS WEEK'S CHALLENGE."]

**VO:** This week, when you encounter "those people" rhetoric — about any group, from any side — do one thing: silently restate the claim about *one real individual* you know or can imagine from that group. "Immigrants are X." Okay — is your specific neighbor, coworker, or classmate X? The accusation usually shrinks the moment it has to fit a real face. That shrinkage is you, refusing to be divided.

---

## RECAP + TEASE

**VO:** Us-vs-them and scapegoating: turn ideas into loyalty, turn complexity into a villain, and turn neighbors into enemies. Watch for loyalty-over-logic, the single simple villain, and dehumanizing words — and always picture the real individual.

[GFX: end card — "NEXT — Technique #15: Fear Appeals & Threat Inflation. Making the danger feel bigger than it is."]

**VO:** Next time: how fear short-circuits judgment, and how a small or imaginary threat gets inflated until you'll accept almost anything to feel safe. See you there.

---

## SOURCES
- In-group/out-group bias and minimal-group experiments; social identity theory: Henri Tajfel and colleagues. See Wikipedia, "Social identity theory."
- Scapegoating overview: Wikipedia, "Scapegoating."
- Inflaming group identities in 2016 operations: Senate Intelligence Committee reports. See Episode 12 sources.

*Full URLs in `SOURCES.md`.*

# Episode 17 — Technique #15: Fear Appeals & Threat Inflation
### Making the danger feel bigger than it is

**Block:** 2 · **Technique 15 of 20** · **Target runtime:** 13–14 min
**Anchor callback:** Campaign B — content tuned to make people feel under threat from each other.
**Logline:** Fear is the fastest way to switch off careful thinking. Manipulators inflate threats — make a small or unlikely danger feel huge and imminent — because frightened people will buy, vote, obey, and surrender freedoms they'd otherwise guard.

**Learning objectives:**
- Understand why fear hijacks judgment faster than any other emotion.
- Recognize *threat inflation*: exaggerating the size, nearness, or likelihood of a danger.
- Learn to right-size a threat before acting on it.

---

## COLD OPEN

[B-ROLL: a late-night ad — ominous music, a shadowy figure at a window, a worried family — then a product: a home alarm, an insurance plan, a supplement.]

**VO:** Ominous music. A shadow at the window. A family that looks just like yours, suddenly in danger. And then, in the last five seconds — relief, for sale. A lock, a pill, a policy, a politician.

[Beat.]

**VO:** First they make you afraid. Then they sell you the cure for the fear they just created. It's the oldest one-two punch there is, and it works because fear is the one emotion your brain refuses to ignore.

[GFX: TITLE CARD — "Technique #15: FEAR APPEALS & THREAT INFLATION."]

---

## SEGMENT 1 — What it is

[ON SCREEN — DEFINITION]
**Fear appeal:** a message that arouses fear to motivate a choice.
**Threat inflation:** exaggerating how big, how near, or how likely a danger is, to make the fear do more work.

**VO:** A fear appeal isn't automatically dishonest — "smoking can kill you" is a fear appeal, and it's true. The manipulation is in the *inflation*: taking a small risk and making it feel enormous, taking a distant risk and making it feel immediate, taking an unlikely event and making it feel certain. And then offering the manipulator's product, candidate, or policy as the only escape.

**VO:** The tell is usually a mismatch between the size of the fear and the size of the actual risk — and a "solution" that conveniently happens to be whatever the messenger was selling anyway.

---

## SEGMENT 2 — Why it works on your brain

[GFX: a brain's "alarm center" lighting up and dimming the "reasoning" area.]

**VO:** Deep in your brain sits an alarm system that evolved to keep you alive. When it senses a threat, it acts *before* your thinking brain catches up — your heart races, your attention narrows, and you get ready to fight, flee, or freeze. That's brilliant when a predator lunges. It's a liability when a marketer or a politician deliberately trips the alarm.

**VO:** Under fear, three things happen that manipulators love. Your thinking narrows to the threat, so you stop considering alternatives. You become *present-focused* and impatient — you want the danger gone *now*, which makes you skip the slow questions. And you become more willing to accept strong authority and harsh measures, because in a real emergency, that can save you. Frighten people enough, and they'll hand over money, votes, privacy, and rights — and feel grateful for the chance.

**VO:** There's also a quirk called the availability shortcut: we judge how likely something is by how easily we can picture it. A single vivid, scary story — replayed over and over — makes a rare danger feel common. The image is doing the lying; the statistics never get a word in.

---

## SEGMENT 3 — In the anchor campaign

[B-ROLL: the troll farm; posts warning each group that the others are coming for them.]

**VO:** Our Campaign B operation ran on inflated fear. Its content told each group that the others were not just wrong but *dangerous* — coming for your rights, your safety, your children, your way of life. The actual likelihood didn't matter. The job was to keep the alarm bell ringing so that people stayed frightened, tribal, and easy to enrage. A frightened citizen is a manageable citizen.

[LOWER THIRD: Threat-laden, fear-driven content · Source: Senate Intelligence Committee reports]

**VO:** Fear and the us-vs-them machine from the last episode work as partners: identify a "them," then inflate the threat that "they" pose. Fear gives the tribalism urgency; the tribe gives the fear a face.

---

## SEGMENT 4 — In the wild today

[B-ROLL: montage — "is your water poisoning your family?"; supplement scares; "crime is out of control" ads; "if they win, the country is finished."]

**VO:** **Advertising.** Whole industries are fear factories: security systems, certain supplements and "detox" products, some insurance pitches. The formula: invent or inflate a danger ("toxins," "germs," "what they're not telling you"), then sell the shield. Local-news teasers — "the common kitchen item that could kill you, tonight at eleven" — keep you watching by keeping you scared.

**VO:** **Politics.** Fear is the campaign trail's most reliable engine. "Crime is exploding." "They'll open the borders / take your guns / destroy the economy." "This is the most important election in history; if we lose, it's over." Sometimes a threat is real and worth raising. The manipulation is the *inflation* — the missing context, the cherry-picked scary statistic, the worst case treated as the only case, the suggestion that catastrophe is certain unless you act exactly as told, right now.

[GFX: a "threat thermometer" — actual risk shown small; the *portrayed* risk balloons far above it.]

**VO:** Watch for the manufactured emergency that demands you skip the normal questions: "There's no time to debate." "We have to act now." Urgency is how fear stops you from thinking — and "act now" is often less about the threat than about beating your slow brain to the decision.

---

## SEGMENT 5 — How to spot it

[GFX: "SPOT-IT" card.]

[ON SCREEN — CHECKLIST: Fear & Threat Inflation]
- **Right-size the risk.** How likely is this, really? How big, how near? Look for actual numbers, not just vivid stories.
- **Cui bono — who sells the cure?** Does the person raising the alarm conveniently sell the solution?
- **Spot the false urgency.** "No time to think" is a reason to think harder, not less.
- **One scary anecdote ≠ a trend.** A single horrifying example tells you the thing *can* happen, not how *often* it does.

**VO:** The defense is to let the fear ring — and then ask the boring questions anyway. How likely? How bad? Compared to what? Who benefits if I'm scared? Real dangers survive those questions; they even get clearer. Inflated ones deflate.

---

## SEGMENT 6 — Try it yourself

[GFX: "THIS WEEK'S CHALLENGE."]

**VO:** This week, catch one fear-based message — an ad, a headline, a political warning — and try to find the actual size of the risk. Search for the real statistics. Ask: out of how many people, how often, compared to what other risks? You'll often find the thing you were told to dread is far rarer than the fear implied — and that the people ringing the bell had something to sell.

---

## RECAP + TEASE

**VO:** Fear appeals and threat inflation: scare you past your reasoning, then sell the cure. Right-size the risk, find who profits from your fear, and treat "no time to think" as the loudest alarm of all.

[GFX: end card — "NEXT — Technique #16: Name-Calling & Demonization. Attacking the person to avoid the argument."]

**VO:** Next time: the cheap, powerful move of destroying a person so you never have to answer their point. See you there.

---

## SOURCES
- Fear appeals in persuasion: long-standing communication research (e.g., the "extended parallel process" model).
- Availability shortcut (judging likelihood by ease of recall): Tversky & Kahneman's work on heuristics.
- Fear-driven content in 2016 operations: Senate Intelligence Committee reports. See Episode 12 sources.

*Full URLs in `SOURCES.md`.*
